world</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/English_as_a_second_language" class="mw-redirect" title="English as a second language">English as a second language</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_English" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:0.6em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English" title="Phonological history of English">Phonological history of English</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nTypes</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:0.6em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">Canadian English</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand English</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nTeaching</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:0.6em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Teaching_English_as_a_second_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Teaching English as a second language">Teaching English as a second language</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:English_words_and_phrases" title="Category:English words and phrases">Category:English words and phrases</a></li></ul>\n<hr />\n<i>Higher category:</i> <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">Language</a></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:English_language" title="Template:English language"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:English_language&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:English language (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:English_language&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>English</b> is a <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="West Germanic language">West Germanic language</a> that was first spoken in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">early medieval England</a> and is now a global <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a6_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a6-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWardhaugh201055_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWardhaugh201055-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Named after the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> that migrated to the area of Britain that would later take their name, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, both names ultimately deriving from the <a href="/wiki/Anglia_(peninsula)" title="Anglia (peninsula)">Anglia</a> peninsula in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. It is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian languages</a>, but its vocabulary has been significantly influenced by other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Norse</a> (a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic language</a>), as well as by <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English has developed over the course of more than 1,400 years. The earliest forms of English, a set of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian dialects</a> brought to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> by <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon settlers</a> in the 5th century, are called <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>. <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> began in the late 11th century with the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> and was a period in which the language was influenced by French.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b30_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b30-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> began in the late 15th century with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> to London, the printing of the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="King James Bible">King James Bible</a> and the start of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through the worldwide influence of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> spread around the world from the 17th to mid-20th centuries. Through all types of printed and electronic media, and spurred by the emergence of the United States as a global <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>, English has become the <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">leading language</a> of international discourse and <a href="/wiki/English_as_a_lingua_franca" title="English as a lingua franca">the <i>lingua franca</i></a> in many regions and professional contexts such as science, navigation and law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">third most spoken native language</a> in the world, after <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is the most widely learned <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> and is either the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> or one of the official languages in <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_English_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where English is an official language">almost 60 sovereign states</a>. There are more people who have learned it as a second language than there are native speakers. English is the most commonly spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and it is widely spoken in some areas of the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, Africa and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108\xe2\x80\x93109_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108\xe2\x80\x93109-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">co-official language of the United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">the European Union</a> and many other world and regional international organisations. It is the most widely spoken Germanic language, accounting for at least 70% of speakers of this Indo-European branch. English has a vast <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, though counting how many words any language has is impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowManyWords2015_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowManyWords2015-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlgeo1999_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlgeo1999-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> English speakers are called "Anglophones".\n</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/English_grammar" title="English grammar">English grammar</a> is the result of a gradual change from a typical Indo-European dependent marking pattern with a rich <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> and relatively free <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> to a mostly <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a> pattern with little <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, a fairly fixed <a href="/wiki/SVO_word_order" class="mw-redirect" title="SVO word order">SVO word order</a> and a complex <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994539_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994539-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> relies more on <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> for the expression of complex <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">passive constructions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interrogative" title="Interrogative">interrogatives</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Negation" title="Negation">negation</a>. Despite noticeable variation among the <a href="/wiki/English_accents" class="mw-redirect" title="English accents">accents</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="English dialects">dialects of English</a> used in different countries and regions\xe2\x80\x94in terms of <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, and sometimes also <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_spelling_differences" class="mw-redirect" title="English spelling differences">spelling</a>\xe2\x80\x94English-speakers from around the world are able to communicate with one another with <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">relative ease</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG/220px-Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG/330px-Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG 1.5x, class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_germanic-languages_2.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b><a href="/wiki/Anglic_languages" title="Anglic languages">Anglic languages</a></b> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFA500; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;English</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF8C00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a></div> <b><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian languages</a></b><br /> Anglic and <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFD700; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian</a></div> <b><a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Germanic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="North Sea Germanic languages">North Sea Germanic languages</a></b> Anglo-Frisian and <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7FFF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German/Low Saxon</a></div> <b><a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic languages</a></b><br /> North Sea Germanic and <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a></div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westgermanic_English_tree.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/250px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png" width="250" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/375px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/500px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="872" data-file-height="457" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westgermanic_English_tree.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing the historical relations between the languages of the West Germanic branch of the Germanic languages</div></div></div>\n<p>English is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European language</a> and belongs to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> group of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199229\xe2\x80\x9330_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199229\xe2\x80\x9330-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">originated</a> from a Germanic tribal and <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">linguistic continuum</a> along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, whose languages are now known as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian</a> subgroup within West Germanic. As such, the modern <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian languages</a> are the closest living relatives of Modern English. <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German/Low Saxon</a> is also closely related, and sometimes English, the Frisian languages, and Low German are grouped together as the <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingvaeonic languages">Ingvaeonic (North Sea Germanic) languages</a>, though this grouping remains debated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199230_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199230-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Old English evolved into <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, which in turn evolved into Modern English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson1992_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson1992-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Particular dialects of Old and Middle English also developed into a number of other <a href="/wiki/Anglic_languages" title="Anglic languages">Anglic languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine198256\xe2\x80\x9365_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine198256\xe2\x80\x9365-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Fingallian" title="Fingallian">Fingallian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forth_and_Bargy_dialect" title="Forth and Bargy dialect">Forth and Bargy (Yola)</a> dialects of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarry198286\xe2\x80\x9387_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarry198286\xe2\x80\x9387-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, the development of English on the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> isolated it from the continental Germanic languages and influences, and has since undergone substantial evolution. English is thus not <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a> with any continental Germanic language, differing in <a href="/wiki/Lexis_(linguistics)" title="Lexis (linguistics)">vocabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, although some, such as Dutch or Frisian, do show strong affinities with English, especially with its earlier stages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarbert2007_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarbert2007-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike Icelandic or Faroese, the long history of invasions of the British Isles by other peoples and languages, particularly <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman French</a>, left a profound mark of their own on the language, such that English shares substantial vocabulary and grammar similarities with many languages outside its linguistic <a href="/wiki/Clades" class="mw-redirect" title="Clades">clades</a>, while also being unintelligible with any of those languages. Some scholars have even argued that English can be considered a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed language</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole</a>\xe2\x80\x94a theory called the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_creole_hypothesis" title="Middle English creole hypothesis">Middle English creole hypothesis</a>. Although the high degree of influence from these languages on the vocabulary and grammar of Modern English is widely acknowledged, most specialists in language contact do not consider English to be a true mixed language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988264\xe2\x80\x93265_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988264\xe2\x80\x93265-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatts2011Chapter_4_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatts2011Chapter_4-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English is classified as a Germanic language because it shares <a href="/wiki/Language_change" title="Language change">innovations</a> with other Germanic languages such as <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurrell2006_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurrell2006-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> These shared innovations show that the languages have descended from a single common ancestor called <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a>. Some shared features of Germanic languages include the use of <a href="/wiki/Modal_verb" title="Modal verb">modal verbs</a>, the division of verbs into <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">strong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">weak</a> classes, and the sound changes affecting <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> consonants, known as <a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_law" title="Grimm&#39;s law">Grimm\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner&#39;s law">Verner\'s laws</a>. English is classified as an Anglo-Frisian language because Frisian and English share other features, such as the <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalisation</a> of consonants that were velar consonants in Proto-Germanic (see <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_Old_English#Palatalization" title="Phonological history of Old English">Phonological history of Old English \xc2\xa7&#160;Palatalization</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nigvan_der_Auwera1994_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nigvan_der_Auwera1994-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>English <i>s<b>i</b>ng</i>, <i>s<b>a</b>ng</i>, <i>s<b>u</b>ng</i>; Dutch <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">z<b>i</b>ngen</i>, <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">z<b>o</b>ng</i>, <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">gez<b>o</b>ngen</i>; German <i lang="de" title="German language text">s<b>i</b>ngen</i>, <i lang="de" title="German language text">s<b>a</b>ng</i>, <i lang="de" title="German language text">ges<b>u</b>ngen</i> (strong verb)</li>\n<li>English <i>laugh</i>, <i>laugh<b>ed</b></i>; Dutch and German <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">lachen</i>, <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">lach<b>te</b></i> (weak verb)</li>\n<li>English <i>foot</i>, Dutch <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">voet</i>, German <i lang="de" title="German language text">Fu\xc3\x9f</i>, Norwegian and Swedish <i lang="sv" title="Swedish language text">fot</i> (initial <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/f/</span> derived from Proto-Indo-European <span title="Proto-Indo-European language" class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;">*p</span> through Grimm\'s law)</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>(Compare Latin <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">pes</i>, <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">stem</a> <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">ped-</i>; Modern Greek <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">\xcf\x80\xcf\x8c\xce\xb4\xce\xb9</span> <i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language transliteration">p\xc3\xb3di</i>; <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <span lang="ru" title="Russian language text">\xd0\xbf\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb4</span> <i lang="ru-Latn" title="Russian-language transliteration">pod</i>; <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span lang="sa" title="Sanskrit language text">\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa5\x8d</span> <i lang="sa-Latn" title="Sanskrit-language transliteration">p\xc3\xa1d</i>)</dd></dl>\n<ul><li>English <i>cheese</i>, Frisian <i lang="fy" title="West Frisian language text">tsiis</i> (<i>ch</i> and <i lang="fy" title="West Frisian language text">ts</i> from palatalisation); German <i lang="de" title="German language text">K\xc3\xa4se</i> and Dutch <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">kaas</i> (<i>k</i> without palatalisation)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_English" title="History of English">History of English</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Germanic_to_Old_English">Proto-Germanic to Old English</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/220px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" width="220" height="369" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/330px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/440px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4098" data-file-height="6868" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The opening to the Old English epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">handwritten</a> in <a href="/wiki/Half-uncial" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-uncial">half-uncial script</a>:<br /><i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">H\xc6\xbf\xc3\xa6t \xc6\xbf\xc4\x93 G\xc4\x81rde/na ing\xc4\x93ar dagum \xc3\xbe\xc4\x93od cyninga / \xc3\xberym ge frunon...</i><br />"Listen! We of the Spear-Danes from days of yore have heard of the glory of the folk-kings..."</div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest form of English is called Old English or Anglo-Saxon (c. 550\xe2\x80\x931066 CE). Old English developed from a set of <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingvaeonic languages">North Sea Germanic dialects</a> originally spoken along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Frisia" title="Frisia">Frisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">Jutland</a>, and Southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> by Germanic tribes known as the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a>. In the fifth century, the Anglo-Saxons <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">settled Britain</a> as <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">the Roman economy and administration collapsed</a>. By the seventh century, the Germanic language of the Anglo-Saxons <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language-death_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language-death in England">became dominant in Britain</a>, replacing the languages of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> (43\xe2\x80\x93409 CE): <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, brought to Britain by the <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar&#39;s invasions of Britain">Roman occupation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollingwoodMyres1936_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollingwoodMyres1936-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddolLeithSwann_et_al.2007_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddolLeithSwann_et_al.2007-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlenchSpriggs1999_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlenchSpriggs1999-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <i>England</i> and <i>English</i> (originally <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc3\x86nglaland</i> and <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc3\x86nglisc</i>) are named after the Angles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworthToller1921_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworthToller1921-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Old English was divided into four dialects: the Anglian dialects, <a href="/wiki/Mercian_dialect" title="Mercian dialect">Mercian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian dialect">Northumbrian</a>, and the Saxon dialects, <a href="/wiki/Kentish_dialect_(Old_English)" title="Kentish dialect (Old English)">Kentish</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Saxon_dialect" title="West Saxon dialect">West Saxon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell19594_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell19594-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Through the educational reforms of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">King Alfred</a> in the ninth century and the influence of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, the West Saxon dialect became the <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard written variety</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToon1992Chapter:_Old_English_Dialects_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToon1992Chapter:_Old_English_Dialects-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> is written in West Saxon, and the earliest English poem, <i><a href="/wiki/C%C3%A6dmon%27s_Hymn" title="C\xc3\xa6dmon&#39;s Hymn">C\xc3\xa6dmon\'s Hymn</a></i>, is written in Northumbrian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonoghue2008_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonoghue2008-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Modern English developed mainly from Mercian, but the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a> developed from Northumbrian. A few short inscriptions from the early period of Old English were written using a <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_runes" title="Anglo-Saxon runes">runic script</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> By the sixth century, a <a href="/wiki/Old_English_Latin_alphabet" title="Old English Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> was adopted, written with <a href="/wiki/Half-uncial" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-uncial">half-uncial</a> <a href="/wiki/Letterform" title="Letterform">letterforms</a>. It included the runic letters <i><a href="/wiki/Wynn" title="Wynn">wynn</a></i> &#x27e8;<i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc6\xbf</i>&#x27e9; and <i><a href="/wiki/Thorn_(letter)" title="Thorn (letter)">thorn</a></i> &#x27e8;<i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc3\xbe</i>&#x27e9;, and the modified Latin letters <i><a href="/wiki/Eth" title="Eth">eth</a></i> &#x27e8;<i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc3\xb0</i>&#x27e9;, and <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%86" title="\xc3\x86">ash</a></i> &#x27e8;<i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">\xc3\xa6</i>&#x27e9;.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDenisonHogg200630\xe2\x80\x9331_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDenisonHogg200630\xe2\x80\x9331-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Old English is very different from Modern English and difficult for 21st-century English speakers to understand. Its grammar was similar to that of modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and its closest relative is <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a>. <a href="/wiki/Old_English_grammar" title="Old English grammar">Nouns, adjectives, pronouns, and verbs</a> had many more <a href="/wiki/Inflectional_morphology" class="mw-redirect" title="Inflectional morphology">inflectional endings and forms</a>, and word order was <a href="/wiki/Pragmatic_word_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Pragmatic word order">much freer</a> than in Modern English. Modern English has <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case forms</a> in pronouns (<i>he</i>, <i>him</i>, <i>his</i>) and a few verb endings (<i>I have</i>, <i>he has</i>), but Old English had case endings in nouns as well, and verbs had more <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> endings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogg1992Chapter_3._Phonology_and_Morphology_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogg1992Chapter_3._Phonology_and_Morphology-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2009_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2009-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETraskTrask2010_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETraskTrask2010-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The translation of Matthew 8:20 from 1000 CE shows examples of case endings (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> plural, <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a> plural, <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> singular) and a verb ending (<a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present</a> plural):\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">Foxas habba\xc3\xb0 holu and heofonan fuglas nest</i></dd>\n<dd>Fox-as habb-a\xc3\xb0 hol-u and heofon-an fugl-as nest-\xe2\x88\x85</dd>\n<dd>fox-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">NOM.PL</span></span> have-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">PRS.PL</span></span> hole-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">ACC.PL</span></span> and heaven-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">GEN.SG</span></span> bird-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">NOM.PL</span></span> nest-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">ACC.PL</span></span></dd>\n<dd>"Foxes have holes and the birds of heaven nests"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass200646\xe2\x80\x9347_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass200646\xe2\x80\x9347-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_English">Middle English</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a></div>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:300px;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style=""><i lang="enm" title="Middle English language text">Englischmen \xc3\xbeeyz hy hadde fram \xc3\xbee bygynnyng \xc3\xbere manner speche, Sou\xc3\xbeeron, Northeron, and Myddel speche in \xc3\xbee myddel of \xc3\xbee lond, \xe2\x80\xa6 No\xc3\xbeeles by comyxstion and mellyng, furst wi\xc3\xbe Danes, and afterward wi\xc3\xbe Normans, in menye \xc3\xbee contray longage ys asperyed, and som vse\xc3\xbe strange wlaffyng, chyteryng, harryng, and garryng grisbytting.</i><br /><br />Although, from the beginning, Englishmen had three manners of speaking, southern, northern and midlands speech in the middle of the country, \xe2\x80\xa6 Nevertheless, through intermingling and mixing, first with Danes and then with Normans, amongst many the country language has arisen, and some use strange stammering, chattering, snarling, and grating gnashing.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/John_of_Trevisa" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Trevisa">John of Trevisa</a>, ca. 1385<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogg2006360\xe2\x80\x93361_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogg2006360\xe2\x80\x93361-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>In the period from the 8th to the 12th century, Old English gradually transformed through <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a> into <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>. Middle English is often arbitrarily defined as beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">conquest of England</a> by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> in 1066, but it developed further in the period from 1200\xe2\x80\x931450.\n</p><p>First, the waves of Norse colonisation of northern parts of the British Isles in the 8th and 9th centuries put Old English into intense contact with <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="North Germanic">North Germanic</a> language. Norse influence was strongest in the Northeastern varieties of Old English spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a> area around York, which was the centre of Norse colonisation; today these features are still particularly present in <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_England_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern England English">Northern English</a>. However the centre of norsified English seems to have been in <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" title="The Midlands">the Midlands</a> around <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lindsey" title="Kingdom of Lindsey">Lindsey</a>, and after 920 CE when Lindsey was reincorporated into the Anglo-Saxon polity, Norse features spread from there into English varieties that had not been in intense contact with Norse speakers. Some elements of Norse influence that persist in all English varieties today are the pronouns beginning with <i>th-</i> (<i>they, them, their</i>) which replaced the Anglo-Saxon pronouns with <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">h-</i> (<i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">hie, him, hera</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988284\xe2\x80\x93290_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988284\xe2\x80\x93290-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> in 1066, the now norsified Old English language was subject to contact with the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norman" title="Old Norman">Old Norman</a> language, a <a href="/wiki/Romance_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance language">Romance language</a> closely related to Modern <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>. The Norman language in England eventually developed into <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a>. Because Norman was spoken primarily by the elites and nobles, while the lower classes continued speaking Anglo-Saxon, the influence of Norman consisted of introducing a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Loanwords" class="mw-redirect" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> related to politics, legislation and prestigious social domains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech200639_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech200639-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Middle English also greatly simplified the inflectional system, probably in order to reconcile Old Norse and Old English, which were inflectionally different but morphologically similar. The distinction between nominative and accusative case was lost except in personal pronouns, the instrumental case was dropped, and the use of the genitive case was limited to describing <a href="/wiki/Possession_(linguistics)" title="Possession (linguistics)">possession</a>. The inflectional system regularised many irregular inflectional forms,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass1992_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass1992-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and gradually simplified the system of agreement, making word order less flexible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischervan_der_Wurff2006111\xe2\x80\x9313_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischervan_der_Wurff2006111\xe2\x80\x9313-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Wycliffe_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Wycliffe Bible">Wycliffe Bible</a> of the 1380s, the passage Matthew 8:20 was written\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i lang="enm" title="Middle English language text">Foxis han dennes, and briddis of heuene han nestis</i><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Here the plural suffix <i lang="enm" title="Middle English language text">-n</i> on the verb <i>have</i> is still retained, but none of the case endings on the nouns are present.\n</p><p>By the 12th century Middle English was fully developed, integrating both Norse and Norman features; it continued to be spoken until the transition to early Modern English around 1500. Middle English literature includes <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">The Canterbury Tales</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malory" title="Thomas Malory">Malory\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Morte_d%27Arthur" title="Le Morte d&#39;Arthur">Le Morte d\'Arthur</a></i>. In the Middle English period, the use of regional dialects in writing proliferated, and dialect traits were even used for effect by authors such as Chaucer.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_English">Early Modern English</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/300px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/450px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/600px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="315" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphic representation of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>, showing how the pronunciation of the long vowels gradually shifted, with the high vowels i: and u: breaking into diphthongs and the lower vowels each shifting their pronunciation up one level</div></div></div>\n<p>The next period in the history of English was Early Modern English (1500\xe2\x80\x931700). Early Modern English was characterised by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a> (1350\xe2\x80\x931700), inflectional simplification, and linguistic standardisation.\n</p><p>The Great Vowel Shift affected the stressed long vowels of Middle English. It was a <a href="/wiki/Chain_shift" title="Chain shift">chain shift</a>, meaning that each shift triggered a subsequent shift in the vowel system. <a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">Mid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">open vowels</a> were <a href="/wiki/Raising_(phonology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Raising (phonology)">raised</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">close vowels</a> were <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">broken</a> into <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>. For example, the word <i>bite</i> was originally pronounced as the word <i>beet</i> is today, and the second vowel in the word <i>about</i> was pronounced as the word <i>boot</i> is today. The Great Vowel Shift explains many irregularities in spelling since English retains many spellings from Middle English, and it also explains why English vowel letters have very different pronunciations from the same letters in other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass2000_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass2000-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEG\xc3\xb6rlach199166\xe2\x80\x9370_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEG\xc3\xb6rlach199166\xe2\x80\x9370-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English began to rise in prestige, relative to Norman French, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>. Around 1430, the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Chancery" title="Court of Chancery">Court of Chancery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a> began using English in its <a href="/wiki/Writ" title="Writ">official documents</a>, and a new standard form of Middle English, known as <a href="/wiki/Chancery_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancery Standard">Chancery Standard</a>, developed from the dialects of London and the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_English" title="East Midlands English">East Midlands</a>. In 1476, <a href="/wiki/William_Caxton" title="William Caxton">William Caxton</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> to England and began publishing the first printed books in London, expanding the influence of this form of English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENevalainenTieken-Boon_van_Ostade2006274\xe2\x80\x9379_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENevalainenTieken-Boon_van_Ostade2006274\xe2\x80\x9379-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Literature from the Early Modern period includes the works of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">translation of the Bible</a> commissioned by <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">King James I</a>. Even after the vowel shift the language still sounded different from Modern English: for example, the <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kn \xc9\xa1n sw/</span> in <i>knight</i>, <i>gnat</i>, and <i>sword</i> were still pronounced. Many of the grammatical features that a modern reader of Shakespeare might find quaint or archaic represent the distinct characteristics of Early Modern English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECercignani1981_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECercignani1981-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1611 King James Version of the Bible, written in Early Modern English, Matthew 8:20 says:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i>The Foxes haue holes and the birds of the ayre haue nests</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass200646\xe2\x80\x9347_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass200646\xe2\x80\x9347-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>This exemplifies the loss of case and its effects on sentence structure (replacement with Subject-Verb-Object word order, and the use of <i>of</i> instead of the non-possessive genitive), and the introduction of loanwords from French (<i>ayre</i>) and word replacements (<i>bird</i> originally meaning "nestling" had replaced OE <i>fugol</i>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_Modern_English">Spread of Modern English</span></h3>\n<p>By the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> had facilitated the spread of English through its colonies and geopolitical dominance. Commerce, science and technology, diplomacy, art, and formal education all contributed to English becoming the first truly global language. English also facilitated worldwide international communication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHow_English_evolved_into_a_global_language2010_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHow_English_evolved_into_a_global_language2010-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> As England continued to form new colonies, these, in turn, became independent and developed their own norms for how to speak and write the language. English was adopted in North America, India, parts of Africa, Australasia, and many other regions. In the post-colonial period, some of the newly created nations that had multiple <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">indigenous languages</a> opted to continue using English as the official language to avoid the political difficulties inherent in promoting any one indigenous language above the others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine2006586_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine2006586-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381\xe2\x80\x9386_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381\xe2\x80\x9386-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In the 20th century the growing economic and cultural influence of the United States and its status as a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a> following the Second World War has, along with worldwide broadcasting in English by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and other broadcasters, significantly accelerated the spread of the language across the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> By the 21st century, English was more widely spoken and written than any language has ever been.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039\xe2\x80\x9310_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039\xe2\x80\x9310-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A major feature in the early development of Modern English was the codification of explicit norms for standard usage, and their dissemination through official media such as public education and state-sponsored publications. In 1755 <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> published his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dictionary_of_the_English_Language" title="A Dictionary of the English Language">A Dictionary of the English Language</a></i> which introduced a standard set of spelling conventions and usage norms. In 1828, <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a> published the <i><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_Dictionary" title="Webster&#39;s Dictionary">American Dictionary of the English language</a></i> in an effort to establish a norm for speaking and writing American English that was independent from the British standard. Within Britain, non-standard or lower class dialect features were increasingly stigmatised, leading to the quick spread of the prestige varieties among the middle classes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991\xe2\x80\x9356_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19991\xe2\x80\x9356-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In terms of grammatical evolution, Modern English has now reached a stage where the loss of case is almost complete (case is now only found in pronouns, such as <i>he</i> and <i>him</i>, <i>she</i> and <i>her</i>, <i>who</i> and <i>whom</i>), and where SVO word-order is mostly fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991\xe2\x80\x9356_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19991\xe2\x80\x9356-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Some changes, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Do-support" title="Do-support">do-support</a> have become universalised. (Earlier English did not use the word "do" as a general auxiliary as Modern English does; at first it was only used in question constructions where it was not obligatory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19992_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19992-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Now, do-support with the verb <i>have</i> is becoming increasingly standardised.) The use of progressive forms in <i>-ing</i>, appears to be spreading to new constructions, and forms such as <i>had been being built</i> are becoming more common. Regularisation of irregular forms also slowly continues (e.g. <i>dreamed</i> instead of <i>dreamt</i>), and analytical alternatives to inflectional forms are becoming more common (e.g. <i>more polite</i> instead of <i>politer</i>). British English is also undergoing change under the influence of American English, fuelled by the strong presence of American English in the media and the prestige associated with the US as a world power. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeechHundtMairSmith200918\xe2\x80\x9319_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeechHundtMairSmith200918\xe2\x80\x9319-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMairLeech2006_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMairLeech2006-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair2006_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair2006-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span></h2>\n<p><span id="Geographic_distribution"></span>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_English_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where English is an official language">List of territorial entities where English is an official language</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_English-speaking_population" title="List of countries by English-speaking population">List of countries by English-speaking population</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">English-speaking world</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_English_native_speakers.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_English_native_speakers.png/260px-Map_of_English_native_speakers.png" width="260" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_English_native_speakers.png/390px-Map_of_English_native_speakers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_English_native_speakers.png/520px-Map_of_English_native_speakers.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="615" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_English_native_speakers.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of English native speakers.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png/260px-Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png" width="260" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png/390px-Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png/520px-Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="601" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_English_speakers_by_country_as_of_2014.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of English speakers by country. <table border="0" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="width:100%; background:none;">  <tbody><tr> <td style="vertical-align: top;"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #225500; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;80\xe2\x80\x93100%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #44aa00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;60\xe2\x80\x9380%</div> </td> <td style="vertical-align: top;"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #66ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;40\xe2\x80\x9360%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #99ff55; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;20\xe2\x80\x9340%</div> </td> <td style="vertical-align: top;"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ccffaa; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;0\xe2\x80\x9320%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b9b9b9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Not available</div> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<p>As of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=English_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 400 million people spoke English as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>, and 1.1 billion spoke it as a secondary language.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> English is probably the third largest language by number of native speakers, after <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> However, when combining native and non-native speakers it may, depending on the estimate used, be the most commonly spoken language in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039\xe2\x80\x9310_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039\xe2\x80\x9310-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> English is spoken by communities on every continent and on oceanic islands in all the major oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b106_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b106-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The countries in which English is spoken can be grouped into different categories by how English is used in each country. The "inner circle"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> countries with many native speakers of English share an international standard of written English and jointly influence speech norms of English around the world. English does not belong to just one country, and it does not belong solely to descendants of English settlers. English is an official language of countries populated by few descendants of native speakers of English. It has also become by far the most important language of international communication when <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">people who share no native language meet anywhere in the world</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_circles_of_English-speaking_countries">Three circles of English-speaking countries</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Braj_Kachru" title="Braj Kachru">Braj Kachru</a> distinguishes countries where English is spoken with a <a href="/wiki/Three_Circles_of_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Three Circles of English">three circles model</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In his model, the "inner circle" countries are countries with large communities of native speakers of English, "outer circle" countries have small communities of native speakers of English but widespread use of English as a second language in education or broadcasting or for local official purposes, and "expanding circle" countries are countries where many learners learn English as a foreign language. Kachru bases his model on the history of how English spread in different countries, how users acquire English, and the range of uses English has in each country. The three circles change membership over time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg" class="image"><img alt="Braj Kachru&#39;s Three Circles of English" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg/220px-Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg/330px-Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg/440px-Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="354" data-file-height="354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kachru%27s_three_circles_of_English.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Braj Kachru\'s <i>Three Circles of English</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Countries with large communities of native speakers of English (the inner circle) include Britain, the United States, Australia, Canada, Ireland, and New Zealand, where the majority speaks English, and South Africa, where a significant minority speaks English. The countries with the most native English speakers are, in descending order, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (at least 231 million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (60 million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffice_for_National_Statistics2013Key_Points_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_for_National_Statistics2013Key_Points-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENational_Records_of_Scotland2013_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENational_Records_of_Scotland2013-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthern_Ireland_Statistics_and_Research_Agency2012Table_KS207NI:_Main_Language_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthern_Ireland_Statistics_and_Research_Agency2012Table_KS207NI:_Main_Language-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Canada (19 million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_Canada2014_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_Canada2014-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (at least 17 million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAustralian_Bureau_of_Statistics2013_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAustralian_Bureau_of_Statistics2013-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> South Africa (4.8 million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_South_Africa2012Table_2.5_Population_by_first_language_spoken_and_province_(number)_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_South_Africa2012Table_2.5_Population_by_first_language_spoken_and_province_(number)-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> (4.2 million), and New Zealand (3.7 million).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_New_Zealand2014_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_New_Zealand2014-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In these countries, children of native speakers learn English from their parents, and local people who speak other languages or new immigrants learn English to communicate in their neighbourhoods and workplaces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The inner-circle countries provide the base from which English spreads to other countries in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estimates of the number of English speakers who are <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> and foreign-language speakers vary greatly from 470 million to more than 1,000 million depending on how proficiency is defined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108\xe2\x80\x93109_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108\xe2\x80\x93109-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Linguist <a href="/wiki/David_Crystal" title="David Crystal">David Crystal</a> estimates that non-native speakers now outnumber native speakers by a ratio of 3 to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In Kachru\'s three-circles model, the "outer circle" countries are countries such as the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERubino2006_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERubino2006-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006a_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006a-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> India, Pakistan, Singapore,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELimAnsaldo2006_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELimAnsaldo2006-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConnell2006_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnell2006-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2007_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2007-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> with a much smaller proportion of native speakers of English but much use of English as a second language for education, government, or domestic business,\nand where English is routinely used for school instruction and official interactions with the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Those countries have millions of native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continua</a> ranging from an <a href="/wiki/English-based_creole_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="English-based creole languages">English-based creole</a> to a more standard version of English. They have many more speakers of English who acquire English in the process of growing up through day by day use and listening to broadcasting, especially if they attend schools where English is the medium of instruction. Varieties of English learned by speakers who are not native speakers born to English-speaking parents may be influenced, especially in their grammar, by the other languages spoken by those learners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Most of those varieties of English include words little used by native speakers of English in the inner-circle countries,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and they may have grammatical and phonological differences from inner-circle varieties as well. The standard English of the inner-circle countries is often taken as a norm for use of English in the outer-circle countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the three-circles model, countries such as Poland, China, Brazil, Germany, Japan, Indonesia, Egypt, and other countries where English is taught as a foreign language make up the "expanding circle".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20084_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20084-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The distinctions between English as a first language, as a second language, and as a foreign language are often debatable and may change in particular countries over time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and some other countries of Europe, knowledge of English as a second language is nearly universal, with over 80 percent of the population able to use it,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission2012_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission2012-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and thus English is routinely used to communicate with foreigners and often in higher education. In these countries, although English is not used for government business, its widespread use puts them at the boundary between the "outer circle" and "expanding circle". English is unusual among world languages in how many of its users are not native speakers but speakers of English as a second or foreign language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006197_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006197-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many users of English in the expanding circle use it to communicate with other people from the expanding circle, so that interaction with native speakers of English plays no part in their decision to use English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006198_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006198-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Non-native varieties of English are widely used for international communication, and speakers of one such variety often encounter features of other varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Very often today a conversation in English anywhere in the world may include no native speakers of English at all, even while including speakers from several different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20087_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20087-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">\n<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">\n<div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 272.37868371333px; border-left-color:#000000"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#000000"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#000000"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 227.1597302733px; border-left-color:#008000"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#008000"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#008000"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px;
