top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 39.592800879773px; border-left-color:#003399"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#003399"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:#003399"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:78.239081057659px 62.278778048811px 0 0; border-top-color:#BF0A30"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:#BF0A30"></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<p>Pie chart showing the percentage of native English speakers living in "inner circle" English-speaking countries. Native speakers are now substantially outnumbered worldwide by second-language speakers of English (not counted in this chart).\n</p>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #BF0A30; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;US (64.3%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #003399; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;UK (16.7%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Canada (5.3%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008751; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Australia (4.7%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FCB514; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;South Africa (1.3%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #008000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Ireland (1.1%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #000000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;New Zealand (1%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:white; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Other (5.6%)</div>\n</div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluricentric_English">Pluricentric English</span></h3>\n<p>English is a <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric language</a>, which means that no one national authority sets the standard for use of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20082_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20082-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine1999_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine1999-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughCable2002_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughCable2002-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20088\xe2\x80\x939_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20088\xe2\x80\x939-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> But English is not a divided language,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081539_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081539-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> despite a long-standing joke originally attributed to <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> that the United Kingdom and the United States are "two countries separated by a common language".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Spoken English, for example English used in broadcasting, generally follows national pronunciation standards that are also established by custom rather than by regulation. International broadcasters are usually identifiable as coming from one country rather than another through their <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">accents</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgill2006_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgill2006-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> but newsreader scripts are also composed largely in international <a href="/wiki/Standard_written_English" title="Standard written English">standard written English</a>. The norms of standard written English are maintained purely by the consensus of educated English-speakers around the world, without any oversight by any government or international organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081537\xe2\x80\x931539_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081537\xe2\x80\x931539-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>American listeners generally readily understand most British broadcasting, and British listeners readily understand most American broadcasting. Most English speakers around the world can understand radio programmes, television programmes, and films from many parts of the English-speaking world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006122_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006122-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Both standard and non-standard varieties of English can include both formal or informal styles, distinguished by word choice and syntax and use both technical and non-technical registers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085\xe2\x80\x936_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085\xe2\x80\x936-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The settlement history of the English-speaking inner circle countries outside Britain helped level dialect distinctions and produce <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koin\xc3\xa9 language">koineised</a> forms of English in South Africa, Australia, and New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006130_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006130-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The majority of immigrants to the United States without British ancestry rapidly adopted English after arrival. Now the majority of the United States population are monolingual English speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006131_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006131-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> although English has been given official status by only 30 of the 50 state governments of the US.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_as_a_global_language">English as a global language</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language_influences_in_English" title="Foreign language influences in English">Foreign language influences in English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Study_of_global_communication" title="Study of global communication">Study of global communication</a></div>\n<p>English has ceased to be an "English language" in the sense of belonging only to people who are ethnically <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19995_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19995-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20061_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20061-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Use of English is growing country-by-country internally and for international communication. Most people learn English for practical rather than ideological reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006195_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006195-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Many speakers of English in Africa have become part of an "Afro-Saxon" language community that unites Africans from different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMazruiMazrui1998_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMazruiMazrui1998-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As decolonisation proceeded throughout the British Empire in the 1950s and 1960s, former colonies often did not reject English but rather continued to use it as independent countries setting their own language policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381\xe2\x80\x9386_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381\xe2\x80\x9386-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesthrie2010594_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesthrie2010594-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> For example, the view of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">English language</a> among many Indians has gone from associating it with colonialism to associating it with economic progress, and English continues to be an official language of India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnnamalai2006_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnnamalai2006-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> English is also widely used in media and literature, and the number of English language books published annually in India is the third largest in the world after the US and UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESailaja20092\xe2\x80\x939_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESailaja20092\xe2\x80\x939-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> However English is rarely spoken as a first language, numbering only around a couple hundred-thousand people, and less than 5% of the population speak fluent English in India.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> David Crystal claimed in 2004 that, combining native and non-native speakers, India now has more people who speak or understand English than any other country in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2004_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2004-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> but the number of English speakers in India is very uncertain, with most scholars concluding that the United States still has more speakers of English than India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2010_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2010-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern English, sometimes described as the first global <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006165_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006165-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> is also regarded as the first <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">world language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006690\xe2\x80\x9391_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006690\xe2\x80\x9391-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> English is the world\'s most widely used language in newspaper publishing, book publishing, international telecommunications, scientific publishing, international trade, mass entertainment, and diplomacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> English is, by international treaty, the basis for the required <a href="/wiki/Controlled_natural_language" title="Controlled natural language">controlled natural languages</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWojcik2006139_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWojcik2006139-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seaspeak" title="Seaspeak">Seaspeak</a> and Airspeak, used as <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international languages</a> of seafaring<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Maritime_Organization2011_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Maritime_Organization2011-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and aviation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Civil_Aviation_Organization2011_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Civil_Aviation_Organization2011-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> English used to have parity with French and German in scientific research, but now it dominates that field.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordin2015_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordin2015-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> It achieved parity with <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> as a language of diplomacy at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> negotiations in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipson200447_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipson200447-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> By the time of the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, English had become pre-eminent <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConradRubal-Lopez1996261_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConradRubal-Lopez1996261-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and is now the main worldwide language of diplomacy and international relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter201229_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter201229-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> It is one of six official languages of the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations2008_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations2008-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Many other worldwide international organisations, including the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>, specify English as a working language or official language of the organisation.\n</p><p>Many regional international organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a>, <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">Association of Southeast Asian Nations</a> (ASEAN),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation</a> (APEC) set English as their organisation\'s sole working language even though most members are not countries with a majority of native English speakers. While the European Union (EU) allows member states to designate any of the national languages as an official language of the Union, in practice English is the main working language of EU organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon2006321_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon2006321-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although in most countries English is not an official language, it is currently the language most often taught as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the countries of the EU, English is the most widely spoken foreign language in nineteen of the twenty-five member states where it is not an official language (that is, the countries other than the UK, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>). In a 2012 official Eurobarometer poll, 38 percent of the EU respondents outside the countries where English is an official language said they could speak English well enough to have a conversation in that language. The next most commonly mentioned foreign language, French (which is the most widely known foreign language in the UK and Ireland), could be used in conversation by 12 percent of respondents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission201221,_19_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission201221,_19-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A working knowledge of English has become a requirement in a number of occupations and professions such as medicine<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlcaraz_ArizaNavarro2006_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlcaraz_ArizaNavarro2006-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and computing. English has become so important in scientific publishing that more than 80 percent of all scientific journal articles indexed by <i>Chemical Abstracts</i> in 1998 were written in English, as were 90 percent of all articles in natural science publications by 1996 and 82 percent of articles in humanities publications by 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006694\xe2\x80\x9395_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006694\xe2\x80\x9395-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Specialised subsets of English arise spontaneously in international communities, for example, among international business people, as an <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">auxiliary language</a>. This has led some scholars to develop the study of English as an auxiliary language. <a href="/wiki/Globish_(Nerriere)" class="mw-redirect" title="Globish (Nerriere)">Globish</a> uses a relatively small subset of English vocabulary (about 1500 words with highest use in international business English) in combination with the standard English grammar. Other examples include <a href="/wiki/Simple_English_Wikipedia" title="Simple English Wikipedia">Simple English</a>.\n</p><p>The increased use of the English language globally has had an effect on other languages, leading to some English words being assimilated into the vocabularies of other languages. This influence of English has led to concerns about <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">language death</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2002_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2002-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and to claims of <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_imperialism" title="Linguistic imperialism">linguistic imperialism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJambor2007_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJambor2007-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and has provoked resistance to the spread of English; however the number of speakers continues to increase because many people around the world think that English provides them with opportunities for better employment and improved lives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006Chapter_12:_English_into_the_Future_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006Chapter_12:_English_into_the_Future-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although some scholars mention a possibility of future divergence of English dialects into mutually unintelligible languages, most think a more likely outcome is that English will continue to function as a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koin\xc3\xa9 language">koineised</a> language in which the standard form unifies speakers from around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2006_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2006-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> English is used as the language for wider communication in countries around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Thus English has grown in worldwide use much more than any <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">constructed language</a> proposed as an <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international auxiliary language</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi2003_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi2003-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006163_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006163-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_phonology" title="English phonology">English phonology</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> of the English language differ from one dialect to another, usually without interfering with mutual communication. Phonological variation affects the inventory of <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> (i.e. speech sounds that distinguish meaning), and phonetic variation consists in differences in pronunciation of the phonemes. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram2006334\xe2\x80\x93335_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram2006334\xe2\x80\x93335-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> This overview mainly describes the <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard pronunciations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>: <a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">Received Pronunciation</a> (RP) and <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> (GA). (See <a href="#Dialects,_accents,_and_varieties">\xc2\xa7&#160;Dialects, accents, and varieties</a>, below.)\n</p><p>The phonetic symbols used below are from the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</a> (IPA).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrHoneybone2007_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrHoneybone2007-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerm\xc3\xbadez-OteroMcMahon2006_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerm\xc3\xbadez-OteroMcMahon2006-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacMahon2006_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacMahon2006-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_phonology#Consonants" title="English phonology">English phonology \xc2\xa7&#160;Consonants</a></div>\n<p>Most English dialects share the same 24<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>consonant phonemes. The consonant inventory shown below is valid for Californian American English,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199941\xe2\x80\x9342_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199941\xe2\x80\x9342-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and for RP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994534_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994534-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">\n\n<caption>Consonant colspan="2">\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">\n</td>\n<td style="border-right: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Voiceless_glottal_fricative" title="Voiceless glottal fricative">h</a></span></td>\n<td style="border-left: 0;">\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">Approximant</a>\n</th>\n<td colspan="2">\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">\n</td>\n<td style="border-right: 0;"></td>\n<td style="border-left: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_lateral_approximants" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar lateral approximants">l</a></span>\n</td>\n<td style="border-right: 0;"></td>\n<td style="border-left: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Alveolar_and_postalveolar_approximants" title="Alveolar and postalveolar approximants">\xc9\xb9</a></span>*\n</td>\n<td style="border-right: 0;"></td>\n<td style="border-left: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">j</a></span>\n</td>\n<td style="border-right: 0;"></td>\n<td style="border-left: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">w</a></span>\n</td>\n<td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><small>* Conventionally transcribed <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span>.</small>\n</p><p>In the table, when <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a> (stops, affricates, and fricatives) appear in pairs, such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p b/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t\xca\x83 d\xca\x92/</span>, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s z/</span>, the first is <a href="/wiki/Fortis_and_lenis" title="Fortis and lenis">fortis</a> (strong) and the second is lenis (weak). Fortis obstruents, such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p t\xca\x83 s/</span> are pronounced with more muscular tension and breath force than lenis consonants, such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b d\xca\x92 z/</span>, and are always <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a>. Lenis consonants are partly <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> at the beginning and end of utterances, and fully voiced between vowels. Fortis stops such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> have additional articulatory or acoustic features in most dialects: they are <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">aspirated</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p\xca\xb0]</span> when they occur alone at the beginning of a stressed syllable, often unaspirated in other cases, and often <a href="/wiki/Unreleased_stop" title="Unreleased stop">unreleased</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p\xcc\x9a]</span> or pre-glottalised <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x94p]</span> at the end of a syllable. In a single-syllable word, a vowel before a fortis stop is shortened: thus <i>nip</i> has a noticeably shorter vowel (phonetically, but not phonemically) than <i>nib</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n\xc9\xaa\xcb\x91b\xcc\xa5]</span> (<a href="#Vowels">see below</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsMees200347\xe2\x80\x9353_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsMees200347\xe2\x80\x9353-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>lenis stops: <i>bin</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b\xcc\xa5\xc9\xaa\xcb\x91n]</span>, <i>about</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x99\xcb\x88ba\xca\x8at]</span>, <i>nib</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n\xc9\xaa\xcb\x91b\xcc\xa5]</span></li>\n<li>fortis stops: <i>pin</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p\xca\xb0\xc9\xaan]</span>; <i>spin</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sp\xc9\xaan]</span>; <i>happy</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88h\xc3\xa6pi]</span>; <i>nip</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n\xc9\xaap\xcc\x9a]</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[n\xc9\xaa\xca\x94p]</span></li></ul>\n<p>In RP, the lateral approximant <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span>, has two main <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a> (pronunciation variants): the clear or plain <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[l]</span>, as in <i>light</i>, and the dark or <a href="/wiki/Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Velarized alveolar lateral approximant">velarised</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xab]</span>, as in <i>full</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200813_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200813-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> GA has dark <i>l</i> in most cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200841_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200841-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>clear <i>l</i>: RP <i>light</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[la\xc9\xaat]</span></li>\n<li>dark <i>l</i>: RP and GA <i>full</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[f\xca\x8a\xc9\xab]</span>, GA <i>light</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xaba\xc9\xaat]</span></li></ul>\n<p>All <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorants</a> (liquids <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l, r/</span> and nasals <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/m, n, \xc5\x8b/</span>) devoice when following a voiceless obstruent, and they are syllabic when following a consonant at the end of a word.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201056\xe2\x80\x9359_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201056\xe2\x80\x9359-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>voiceless sonorants: <i>clay</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[kl\xcc\xa5e\xc9\xaa\xcc\xaf]</span>; <i>snow</i> RP <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sn\xcc\xa5\xc9\x99\xca\x8a\xcc\xaf]</span>, GA <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sn\xcc\xa5o\xca\x8a\xcc\xaf]</span></li>\n<li>syllabic sonorants: <i>paddle</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88pad.l\xcc\xa9]</span>, <i>button</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88b\xca\x8ct.n\xcc\xa9]</span></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_phonology#Vowels" title="English phonology">English phonology \xc2\xa7&#160;Vowels</a></div>\n<p>The pronunciation of vowels varies a great deal between dialects and is one of the most detectable aspects of a speaker\'s accent. The table below lists the vowel <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> in Received Pronunciation (RP) and General American (GA), with examples of words in which they occur from <a href="/wiki/Lexical_set" title="Lexical set">lexical sets</a> compiled by linguists. The vowels are represented with symbols from the International Phonetic Alphabet; those given for RP are standard in British dictionaries and other publications.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; float: left; margin-right: vowel">\xca\x8a</a></span></td>\n<td>g<b>oo</b>d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90</a></span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">\xc9\x99</a></span></td>\n<td>comm<b>a</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="float: Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e\xc9\xaa</span></td>\n<td>b<b>ay</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x99\xca\x8a</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">o\xca\x8a</span></td>\n<td>r<b>oa</b>d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a\xc9\xaa</span></td>\n<td>cr<b>y</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a\xca\x8a</span></td>\n<td>c<b>ow</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x94\xc9\xaa</span></td>\n<td>b<b>oy</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">\n<caption>Centering diphthongs\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">RP</a></th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">GA</a></th>\n<th>word\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\xaa\xc9\x99</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\xaa\xc9\xb9</span></td>\n<td>p<b>eer</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e\xc9\x99</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x9b\xc9\xb9</span></td>\n<td>p<b>air</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xca\x8a\xc9\x99</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xca\x8a\xc9\xb9</span></td>\n<td>p<b>oor</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>In RP, vowel length is phonemic; <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long vowels</a> are marked with a <a href="/wiki/Triangular_colon" class="mw-redirect" title="Triangular colon">triangular colon</a> &#x27e8;<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xcb\x90</span>&#x27e9; in the table above, such as the vowel of <i>need</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ni\xcb\x90d]</span> as opposed to <i>bid</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b\xc9\xaad]</span>. In GA, vowel length is non-distinctive.\n</p><p>In both RP and GA, vowels are phonetically <a href="/wiki/Clipping_(phonetics)" title="Clipping (phonetics)">shortened before fortis consonants</a> in the same <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a>, like <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t t\xca\x83 f/</span>, but not before lenis consonants like <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d d\xca\x92 v/</span> or in open syllables: thus, the vowels of <i>rich</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[r\xc9\xaat\xca\x83]</span>, <i>neat</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[nit]</span>, and <i>safe</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[se\xc9\xaa\xcc\xaff]</span> are noticeably shorter than the vowels of <i>ridge</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[r\xc9\xaa\xcb\x91d\xca\x92]</span>, <i>need</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ni\xcb\x91d]</span>, and <i>save</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[se\xcb\x91\xc9\xaa\xcc\xafv]</span>, and the vowel of <i>light</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[la\xc9\xaa\xcc\xaft]</span> is shorter than that of <i>lie</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[la\xcb\x91\xc9\xaa\xcc\xaf]</span>. Because lenis consonants are frequently voiceless at the end of a syllable, vowel length is an important cue as to whether the following consonant is lenis or fortis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollinsMees200346\xe2\x80\x9350_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollinsMees200346\xe2\x80\x9350-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99/</span> only occurs in unstressed syllables and is closer in quality when followed by a morpheme-internal consonant and opener when morpheme-final or prevocalic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECruttenden2014138_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECruttenden2014138-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemmingJohnson2007_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemmingJohnson2007-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Some dialects do not contrast <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\xaa/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99/</span> in unstressed positions, so that <i>rabbit</i> and <i>abbot</i> rhyme and <i>Lenin</i> and <i>Lennon</i> are homophonous, a dialect feature called <a href="/wiki/Weak_vowel_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak vowel merger">weak vowel merger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982167_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982167-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> GA <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x9cr/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99r/</span> are realised as an <a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel" title="R-colored vowel"><i>r</i>-coloured vowel</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x9a]</span>, as in <i>further</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88f\xc9\x9a\xc3\xb0\xc9\x9a]</span> (phonemically <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xcb\x88f\xc9\x9cr\xc3\xb0\xc9\x99r/</span>, which in RP is realised as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88f\xc9\x99\xcb\x90\xc3\xb0\xc9\x99]</span> (phonemically <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xcb\x88f\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90\xc3\xb0\xc9\x99/</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982121_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982121-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonotactics">Phonotactics</span></h3>\n<p>An English syllable includes a syllable nucleus consisting of a vowel sound. Syllable onset and coda (start and end) are optional. A syllable can start with up to three consonant sounds, as in <i>sprint</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/spr\xc9\xaant/</span>, and end with up to four, as in <i>texts</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/teksts/</span>. This gives an English syllable the following structure, (CCC)V(CCCC) where C represents a consonant and V a vowel; the word <i>strengths</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/str\xc9\x9b\xc5\x8bk\xce\xb8s/</span> is thus an example of the most complex syllable possible in English. The consonants that may appear together in onsets or codas are restricted, as is the order in which they may appear. Onsets can only have four types of consonant clusters: a stop and approximant, as in <i>play</i>; a voiceless fricative and approximant, as in <i>fly</i> or <i>sly</i>; <i>s</i> and a voiceless stop, as in <i>stay</i>; and <i>s</i>, a voiceless stop, and an approximant, as in <i>string</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201060_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201060-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Clusters of nasal and stop are only allowed in codas. Clusters of obstruents always agree invoicing, and clusters of sibilants and of plosives with the same point of articulation are prohibited. Furthermore, several consonants have limited distributions: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/h/</span> can only occur in syllable-initial position, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc5\x8b/</span> only in syllable-final position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994537\xe2\x80\x93538_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994537\xe2\x80\x93538-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Stress.2C_rhythm_and_intonation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress,_rhythm_and_intonation">Stress, rhythm and intonation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stress_and_vowel_reduction_in_English" title="Stress and vowel reduction in English">Stress and vowel reduction in English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intonation_in_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Intonation in English">Intonation in English</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">Stress</a> plays an important role in English. Certain <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> are stressed, while others are unstressed. Stress is a combination of duration, intensity, vowel quality, and sometimes changes in pitch. Stressed syllables are pronounced longer and louder than unstressed syllables, and vowels in unstressed syllables are frequently <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">reduced</a> while vowels in stressed syllables are not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199942_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199942-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Some words, primarily short function words but also some modal verbs such as <i>can</i>, have <a href="/wiki/Weak_and_strong_forms_in_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Weak and strong forms in English">weak and strong forms</a> depending on whether they occur in stressed or non-stressed position within a sentence.\n</p><p>Stress in English is <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemic</a>, and some pairs of words are distinguished by stress. For instance, the word <i>contract</i> is stressed on the first syllable (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">KON</span>-trakt</i></a>) when used as a noun, but on the last syllable (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">k\xc9\x99n-<span style="font-size:90%">TRAKT</span></i></a>) for most meanings (for example, "reduce in size") when used as a verb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOxford_Learner&#39;s_Dictionary2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOxford_Learner&#39;s_Dictionary2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam_Webster2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam_Webster2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacquarie_Dictionary2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacquarie_Dictionary2015Entry_&quot;contract&quot;-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Here stress is connected to <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">vowel reduction</a>: in the noun "contract" the first syllable is stressed and has the unreduced vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x92/</span>, but in the verb "contract" the first syllable is unstressed and its vowel is reduced to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99/</span>. Stress is also used to distinguish between words and phrases, so that a compound word receives a single stress unit, but the corresponding phrase has two: e.g. <i>a burnout</i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90r/: &#39;ur&#39; in &#39;fur&#39;">\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90r</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/a\xca\x8a/: &#39;ou&#39; in &#39;mouth&#39;">a\xca\x8a</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>) versus <i>to burn out</i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90r/: &#39;ur&#39; in &#39;fur&#39;">\xc9\x9c\xcb\x90r</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/a\xca\x8a/: &#39;ou&#39; in &#39;mouth&#39;">a\xca\x8a</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>), and <i>a hotdog</i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span></span>/</a></span></span>) versus <i>a hot dog</i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span></span>/</a></span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201066_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrintonBrinton201066-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In terms of <a href="/wiki/Rhythm" title="Rhythm">rhythm</a>, English is generally described as a <a href="/wiki/Stress-timed" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress-timed">stress-timed</a> language, meaning that the amount of time between stressed syllables tends to be equal. Stressed syllables are pronounced longer, but unstressed syllables (syllables between stresses) are shortened. Vowels in unstressed syllables are shortened as well, and vowel shortening causes changes in <a href="/wiki/Vowel_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Vowel quality">vowel quality</a>: <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">vowel reduction</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variation">Regional variation</span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94/</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91/</span></td>\n<td rowspan="4"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91/</span></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot\xe2\x80\x93caught title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x92/</span></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lot%E2%80%93cloth_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Lot\xe2\x80\x93cloth title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91/</span></td>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Father%E2%80%93bother_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Father\xe2\x80\x93bother merger"><i>father</i>\xe2\x80\x93<i>bother</i> merger</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">PALM</span>\n</th>\n<td rowspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">BATH</span>\n</th>\n<td rowspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6/</span></td>\n<td rowspan="2"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6/</span></td>\n<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Trap%E2%80%93bath_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Trap\xe2\x80\x93bath split"><i>trap</i>\xe2\x80\x93<i>bath</i> split</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">TRAP</span>\n</th>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6/</span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Varieties of English vary the most in pronunciation of vowels. The best known national varieties used as standards for education in non English-speaking countries are British (BrE) and American (AmE). Countries such as <a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiberno_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno English">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South Africa</a> have their own standard varieties which are less often used as standards for education internationally. Some differences between the various dialects are shown in the table "Varieties of Standard English and their features".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20024\xe2\x80\x936_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20024\xe2\x80\x936-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English has undergone many <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English" title="Phonological history of English">historical sound changes</a>, some of them affecting all varieties, and others affecting only a few. Most standard varieties are affected by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>, which changed the pronunciation of long vowels, but a few dialects have slightly different results. In North America, a number of chain shifts such as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Cities_Vowel_Shift" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Cities Vowel Shift">Northern Cities Vowel Shift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shift" title="Canadian Shift">Canadian Shift</a> have produced very different vowel landscapes in some regional accents.\n</p><p>Some dialects have fewer or more consonant phonemes and <a href="/wiki/Phone_(phonetics)" title="Phone (phonetics)">phones</a> than the standard varieties. Some conservative varieties like Scottish English have a <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labialized_velar_approximant" title="Voiceless labialized velar approximant">\xca\x8d</a>]</span> sound in <i>whine</i> that contrasts with the voiced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w]</span> in <i>wine</i>, but most other dialects pronounce both words with voiced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w]</span>, a dialect feature called <a href="/wiki/Wine%E2%80%93whine_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Wine\xe2\x80\x93whine merger"><i>wine</i>\xe2\x80\x93<i>whine</i> merger</a>. The unvoiced velar fricative sound <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/x/</span> is found in Scottish English, which distinguishes <i>loch</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l\xc9\x94x/</span> from <i>lock</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l\xc9\x94k/</span>. Accents like <a href="/wiki/Cockney" title="Cockney">Cockney</a> with "<i>h</i>-dropping" lack the glottal fricative <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/h/</span>, and dialects with <a href="/wiki/Th-stopping" title="Th-stopping"><i>th</i>-stopping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Th-fronting" title="Th-fronting"><i>th</i>-fronting</a> like <a href="/wiki/African_American_Vernacular_English" class="mw-redirect" title="African American Vernacular English">African American Vernacular</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estuary_English" title="Estuary English">Estuary English</a> do not have the dental fricatives <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xce\xb8, \xc3\xb0/</span>, but replace them with dental or alveolar stops <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t, d/</span> or labiodental fricatives <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/f, v/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoach200953_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoach200953-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiegerich199236_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiegerich199236-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Other changes affecting the phonology of local varieties are processes such as <a href="/wiki/Yod-dropping" class="mw-redirect" title="Yod-dropping"><i>yod</i>-dropping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yod-coalescence" class="mw-redirect" title="Yod-coalescence"><i>yod</i>-coalescence</a>, and reduction of consonant clusters.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">Received Pronunciation</a> vary in their pronunciation of historical <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> after a vowel at the end of a syllable (in the <a href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable coda">syllable coda</a>). GA is a <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">rhotic dialect</a>, meaning that it pronounces <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> at the end of a syllable, but RP is non-rhotic, meaning that it loses <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> in that position. English dialects are classified as rhotic or non-rhotic depending on whether they elide <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> like RP or keep it like GA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass2000114_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass2000114-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is complex dialectal variation in words with the <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English_short_A" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonological history of English short A">open front</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English_low_back_vowels" title="Phonological history of English low back vowels">open back vowels</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6 \xc9\x91\xcb\x90 \xc9\x92 \xc9\x94\xcb\x90/</span>. These four vowels are only distinguished in RP, Australia, New Zealand and South Africa. In GA, these vowels merge to three <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6 \xc9\x91 \xc9\x94/</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982xviii-xix_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982xviii-xix-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> and in Canadian English, they merge to two <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6 \xc9\x91/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells1982493_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells1982493-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the words that have each vowel vary by dialect. The table "Dialects and open vowels" shows this variation with <a href="/wiki/Lexical_set" title="Lexical set">lexical sets</a> in which these sounds occur.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_grammar" title="English grammar">English grammar</a></div>\n<p>As is typical of an Indo-European language, English follows <a href="/wiki/Nominative%E2%80%93accusative_language" title="Nominative\xe2\x80\x93accusative language">accusative</a> <a href="/wiki/Morphosyntactic_alignment" title="Morphosyntactic alignment">morphosyntactic alignment</a>. Unlike other Indo-European languages though, English has largely abandoned the inflectional <a href="/wiki/Case_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Case system">case system</a> in favor of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a> constructions. Only the <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a> retain morphological case more strongly than any other <a href="/wiki/Word_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Word class">word class</a>. English distinguishes at least seven major word classes: verbs, nouns, adjectives, adverbs, determiners (including articles), prepositions, and conjunctions. Some analyses add pronouns as a class separate from nouns, and subdivide conjunctions into subordinators and coordinators, and add the class of interjections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200222_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200222-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> English also has a rich set of auxiliary verbs, such as <i>have</i> and <i>do</i>, expressing the categories of mood and aspect. Questions are marked by <a href="/wiki/Do-support" title="Do-support">do-support</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wh-movement" title="Wh-movement">wh-movement</a> (fronting of question words beginning with <i>wh</i>-) and word order <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93auxiliary_inversion" title="Subject\xe2\x80\x93auxiliary inversion">inversion</a> with some verbs.\n</p><p>Some traits typical of Germanic languages persist in English, such as the distinction between irregularly inflected <a href="/wiki/Strong_inflection" title="Strong inflection">strong</a> stems inflected through <a href="/wiki/Ablaut" class="mw-redirect" title="Ablaut">ablaut</a> (i.e. changing the vowel of the stem, as in the pairs <i>speak/spoke</i> and <i>foot/feet</i>) and weak stems inflected through affixation (such as <i>love/loved</i>, <i>hand/hands</i>). Vestiges of the case and gender system are found in the pronoun system (<i>he/him, who/whom</i>) and in the inflection of the copula verb <i>to be</i>.\n</p><p>The seven word classes are exemplified in this sample sentence:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAartsHaegeman2006118_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAartsHaegeman2006118-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="text-align: center;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><i>The</i></td>\n<td><i>chairman</i></td>\n<td><i>of</i></td>\n<td><i>the</i></td>\n<td><i>committee</i></td>\n<td><i>and</i></td>\n<td><i>the</i></td>\n<td><i>loquacious</i></td>\n<td><i>politician</i></td>\n<td><i>clashed</i></td>\n<td><i>violently</i></td>\n<td><i>when</i></td>\n<td><i>the</i></td>\n<td><i>meeting</i></td>\n<td><i>started</i>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Det.</td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Prep.</td>\n<td>Det.</td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Conj.</td>\n<td>Det.</td>\n<td>Adj.</td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Verb</td>\n<td>Advb.</td>\n<td>Conj.</td>\n<td>Det.</td>\n<td>Noun</td>\n<td>Verb\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns_and_noun_phrases">Nouns and noun phrases</span></h3>\n<p>English nouns are only inflected for number and possession. New nouns can be formed through derivation or compounding. They are semantically divided into <a href="/wiki/Proper_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Proper nouns">proper nouns</a> (names) and common nouns. Common nouns are in turn divided into concrete and abstract nouns, and grammatically into <a href="/wiki/Count_noun" title="Count noun">count nouns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass nouns">mass nouns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayneHuddleston2002_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayneHuddleston2002-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most count nouns are inflected for plural number through the use of the plural <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> -<i>s</i>, but a few nouns have irregular plural forms. Mass nouns can only be pluralised through the use of a count noun classifier, e.g. <i>one loaf of bread</i>, <i>two loaves of bread</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200256\xe2\x80\x9357_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200256\xe2\x80\x9357-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regular plural formation:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Singular: <i>cat, dog</i></dd>\n<dd>Plural: <i>cats, dogs</i></dd></dl>\n<p>Irregular plural formation:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Singular: <i>man, woman, foot, fish, ox, knife, mouse</i></dd>\n<dd>Plural: <i>men, women, feet, fish, oxen, knives, mice</i></dd></dl>\n<p>Possession can be expressed either by the possessive <a href="/wiki/Enclitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclitic">enclitic</a> -<i>s</i> (also traditionally called a genitive suffix), or by the preposition <i>of</i>. Historically the -s possessive has been used for animate nouns, whereas the <i>of</i> possessive has been reserved for inanimate nouns. Today this distinction is less clear, and many speakers use -<i>s</i> also with inanimates. Orthographically the possessive -s is separated from the noun root with an apostrophe.\n</p><p>Possessive constructions:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>With -s: <i>The woman\'s husband\'s child</i></dd>\n<dd>With of: <i>The child of the husband of the woman</i></dd></dl>\n<p>Nouns can form <a href="/wiki/Noun_phrase" title="Noun phrase">noun phrases</a> (NPs) where they are the syntactic head of the words that depend on them such as determiners, quantifiers, conjunctions or adjectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200255_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200255-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Noun phrases can be short, such as <i>the man</i>, composed only of a determiner and a noun. They can also include modifiers such as adjectives (e.g. <i>red</i>, <i>tall</i>, <i>all</i>) and specifiers such as determiners (e.g. <i>the</i>, <i>that</i>). But they can also tie together several nouns into a single long NP, using conjunctions such as <i>and</i>, or prepositions such as <i>with</i>, e.g. <i>the tall man with the long red trousers and his skinny wife with the spectacles</i> (this NP uses conjunctions, prepositions, specifiers, and modifiers). Regardless of length, an NP functions as a syntactic unit. For example, the possessive enclitic can, in cases which do not lead to ambiguity, follow the entire noun phrase, as in <i>The President of India\'s wife</i>, where the enclitic follows <i>India</i> and not <i>President</i>.\n</p><p>The class of determiners is used to specify the noun they precede in terms of <a href="/wiki/Definiteness" title="Definiteness">definiteness</a>, where <i>the</i> marks a definite noun and <i>a</i> or <i>an</i> an indefinite one. A definite noun is assumed by the speaker to be already known by the interlocutor, whereas an indefinite noun is not specified as being previously known. Quantifiers, which include <i>one</i>, <i>many</i>, <i>some</i> and <i>all</i>, are used to specify the noun in terms of quantity or number. The noun must agree with the number of the determiner, e.g. <i>one man</i> (sg.) but <i>all men</i> (pl.). Determiners are the first constituents in a noun phrase.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200254\xe2\x80\x935_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200254\xe2\x80\x935-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">Adjectives</span></h4>\n<p>Adjectives modify a noun by providing additional information about their referents. In English, adjectives come before the nouns they modify and after determiners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200257_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum200257-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In Modern English, adjectives are not inflected, and they do not <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">agree</a> in form with the noun they modify, as adjectives in most other Indo-European languages do. For example, in the phrases <i>the slender boy</i>, and <i>many slender girls</i>, the adjective <i>slender</i> does not change form to agree with either the number or gender of the noun.\n</p><p>Some adjectives are inflected for <a href="/wiki/Comparison_(grammar)" title="Comparison (grammar)">degree of comparison</a>, with the positive degree unmarked, the suffix <i>-er</i> marking the comparative, and <i>-est</i> marking the superlative: <i>a small boy</i>, <i>the boy is smaller than the girl</i>, <i>that boy is the smallest</i>. Some adjectives have irregular comparative and superlative forms, such as <i>good</i>, <i>better</i>, and <i>best</i>. Other adjectives have comparatives formed by <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastic constructions</a>, with the adverb <i>more</i> marking the comparative, and <i>most</i> marking the superlative: <i>happier</i> or <i>more happy</i>, <i>the happiest</i> or <i>most happy</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994540_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994540-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> There is some variation among speakers regarding which adjectives use inflected or periphrastic comparison, and some studies have shown a tendency for the periphrastic forms to become more common at the expense of the inflected form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair2006148\xe2\x80\x9349_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair2006148\xe2\x80\x9349-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Pronouns.2C_case.2C_and_person"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns,_case,_and_person">Pronouns, case, and person</span></h4>\n<p>English pronouns conserve many traits of case and gender inflection. The personal pronouns retain a difference between subjective and objective case in most persons (<i>I/me, he/him, she/her, we/us, they/them</i>) as well as a gender and animateness distinction in the third person singular (distinguishing <i>he/she/it</i>). The <a href="/wiki/Subjective_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Subjective case">subjective case</a> corresponds to the Old English <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Objective_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Objective case">objective case</a> is used both in the sense of the previous <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative case</a> (in the role of patient, or direct object of a transitive verb), and in the sense of the Old English dative case (in the role of a recipient or <a href="/wiki/Indirect_object" class="mw-redirect" title="Indirect object">indirect object</a> of a transitive verb).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeech200669_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeech200669-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Dwyer2006_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Dwyer2006-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Subjective case is used when the pronoun is the subject of a finite clause, and otherwise, the objective case is used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenbaumNelson2002_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenbaumNelson2002-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> While grammarians such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Sweet" title="Henry Sweet">Henry Sweet</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESweet201452_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESweet201452-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Jespersen" title="Otto Jespersen">Otto Jespersen</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJespersen2007173-185_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJespersen2007173-185-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> noted that the English cases did not correspond to the traditional Latin based system, some contemporary grammars, for example <a href="#CITEREFHuddlestonPullum2002">Huddleston &amp; Pullum (2002)</a>, retain traditional labels for the cases, calling them nominative and accusative cases respectively.\n</p><p>Possessive pronouns exist in dependent and independent forms; the dependent form functions as a determiner specifying a noun (as in <i>my chair</i>), while the independent form can stand alone as if it were a noun (e.g. <i>the chair is mine</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum2002425\xe2\x80\x9326_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum2002425\xe2\x80\x9326-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The English system of grammatical person no longer has a distinction between formal and informal pronouns of address (the old 2nd person singular familiar pronoun <i><a href="/wiki/Thou" title="Thou">thou</a></i> acquired a pejorative or inferior tinge of meaning and was abandoned), and the forms for 2nd person plural and singular are identical except in the reflexive form. Some dialects have introduced innovative 2nd person plural pronouns such as <i>y\'all</i> found in <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_American_Vernacular_English" class="mw-redirect" title="African American Vernacular English">African American (Vernacular) English</a> or <i>youse</i> and <i>ye</i> found in Irish English.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<caption>English personal pronouns\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col">Person\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Subjective case\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Objective case\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Dependent possessive\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Independent possessive\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Reflexive\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1st p. sg.\n</th>\n<td><i>I</i></td>\n<td><i>me</i></td>\n<td><i>my</i></td>\n<td><i>mine</i></td>\n<td><i>myself</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2nd p. sg.\n</th>\n<td><i>you</i></td>\n<td><i>you</i></td>\n<td><i>your</i></td>\n<td><i>yours</i></td>\n<td><i>yourself</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">3rd p. sg.\n</th>\n<td><i>he/she/it</i></td>\n<td><i>him/her/it</i></td>\n<td><i>his/her/its</i></td>\n<td><i>his/hers/its</i></td>\n<td><i>himself/herself/itself</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">1st p. pl.\n</th>\n<td><i>we</i></td>\n<td><i>us</i></td>\n<td><i>our</i></td>\n<td><i>ours</i></td>\n<td><i>ourselves</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">2nd p. pl.\n</th>\n<td><i>you</i></td>\n<td><i>you</i></td>\n<td><i>your</i></td>\n<td><i>yours</i></td>\n<td><i>yourselves</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">3rd p. pl\n</th>\n<td><i>they</i></td>\n<td><i>them</i></td>\n<td><i>their</i></td>\n<td><i>theirs</i></td>\n<td><i>themselves</i>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Pronouns are used to refer to entities <a href="/wiki/Deixis" title="Deixis">deictically</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anaphoric_reference" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphoric reference">anaphorically</a>. A deictic pronoun points to some person or object by identifying it relative to the speech situation\xe2\x80\x94for example, the pronoun <i>I</i> identifies the speaker, and the pronoun <i>you</i>, the addressee. Anaphorical pronouns such as <i>that</i> refer back to an entity already mentioned or assumed by the speaker to be known by the audience, for example in the sentence <i>I already told you that</i>. The reflexive pronouns are used when the oblique argument is identical to the subject of a phrase (e.g. "he sent it to himself" or "she braced herself for impact").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum2002426_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddlestonPullum2002426-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prepositions">Prepositions</span></h4>\n<p>Prepositional phrases (PP) are phrases composed of a preposition and one or more nouns, e.g. <i>with the dog</i>, <i>for my friend</i>, <i>to school</i>, <i>in England</i>. Prepositions have a wide range of uses in English. They are used to describe movement, place, and other relations between different entities, but they also have many syntactic uses such as introducing complement clauses and oblique arguments of verbs. For example, in the phrase <i>I gave it to him</i>, the preposition <i>to</i> marks the recipient, or Indirect Object of the verb <i>to give</i>. Traditionally words were only considered prepositions if they governed the case of the noun they preceded, for example causing the pronouns to use the objective rather than subjective form, "with her", "to me", "for us". But some contemporary grammars such as that of <a href="#CITEREFHuddlestonPullum2002">Huddleston &amp; Pullum (2002</a>:598\xe2\x80\x93600) no longer consider government of case to be the defining feature of the class of prepositions, rather defining prepositions as words that can function as the heads of prepositional phrases.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs_and_verb_phrases">Verbs and verb phrases</span></h3>\n<p>English verbs are inflected for tense and aspect and marked for agreement with present-tense third-person singular subject. Only the copula verb <i>to be</i> is still inflected for agreement with the plural and first and second person subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994540_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK\xc3\xb6nig1994540-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Auxiliary verbs such as <i>have</i> and <i>be</i> are paired with verbs in the infinitive, past, or progressive forms. They form <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">complex</a> tenses, aspects, and moods.
