See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Non-RhoticityUSA.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png/220px-Non-RhoticityUSA.png" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png/330px-Non-RhoticityUSA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Non-RhoticityUSA.png 2x" data-file-width="392" data-file-height="557" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Non-RhoticityUSA.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">Rhoticity</a> dominates in <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlas_of_North_American_English" title="The Atlas of North American English">The Atlas of North American English</a></i> found over 50% <i>non</i>-rhoticity, though, in at least one local white speaker in each U.S. metropolitan area designated here by a red dot. Non-rhotic <a href="/wiki/African_American_Vernacular_English" class="mw-redirect" title="African American Vernacular English">African American Vernacular English</a> pronunciations may be found among <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> regardless of location.</div></div></div>\n<p>North American English is fairly homogeneous compared to British English. Today, American accent variation is often increasing at the regional level and decreasing at the very local level,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELabov2012_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELabov2012-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> though most Americans still speak within a phonological continuum of similar accents,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWells198234_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells198234-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> known collectively as <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> (GA), with differences hardly noticed even among Americans themselves (such as <a href="/wiki/Midland_American_English" title="Midland American English">Midland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western American English</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowicka2006_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowicka2006-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToon1982_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToon1982-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECassidy1982_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECassidy1982-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In most American and Canadian English dialects, <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">rhoticity</a> (or <i>r</i>-fulness) is dominant, with non-rhoticity (<i>r</i>-dropping) becoming associated with lower prestige and social class especially after World War II; this contrasts with the situation in England, where non-rhoticity has become the standard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELabov1972_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELabov1972-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Separate from GA are American dialects with clearly distinct sound systems, historically including <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a>, English of the coastal Northeast (famously including <a href="/wiki/Eastern_New_England_English" title="Eastern New England English">Eastern New England English</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City English</a>), and <a href="/wiki/African_American_Vernacular_English" class="mw-redirect" title="African American Vernacular English">African American Vernacular English</a>, all of which are historically non-rhotic. <a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">Canadian English</a>, except for the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_provinces" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic provinces">Atlantic provinces</a> and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, may be classified under GA as well, but it often shows the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">raising of the vowels</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/a\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">a\xc9\xaa</span></span>/</a></span></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/a\xca\x8a/: &#39;ou&#39; in &#39;mouth&#39;">a\xca\x8a</span></span>/</a></span></span> before <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_consonants" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless consonants">voiceless consonants</a>, as well as distinct norms for written and pronunciation standards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoberg2010_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoberg2010-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a>, the most populous American "accent group" outside of GA,<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> rhoticity now strongly prevails, replacing the region\'s <a href="/wiki/Older_Southern_American_English" title="Older Southern American English">historical non-rhotic prestige</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevineCrockett1966_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevineCrockett1966-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESch\xc3\xb6nweitz2001_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESch\xc3\xb6nweitz2001-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Southern accents are colloquially described as a "drawl" or "twang,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMontgomery1993_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMontgomery1993-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> being recognised most readily by the Southern Vowel Shift initiated by <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">glide-deleting</a> in the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xc9\xaa/</span> vowel (e.g. pronouncing <i>spy</i> almost like <i>spa</i>), the "Southern breaking" of several front pure vowels into a gliding vowel or even two syllables (e.g. pronouncing the word "press" almost like "pray-us"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas200895\xe2\x80\x9396_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas200895\xe2\x80\x9396-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Pin%E2%80%93pen_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Pin\xe2\x80\x93pen merger">pin\xe2\x80\x93pen merger</a>, and other distinctive phonological, grammatical, and lexical features, many of which are actually recent developments of the 19th century or later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey1997_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey1997-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today spoken primarily by working- and middle-class <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/African-American_Vernacular_English" title="African-American Vernacular English">African-American Vernacular English</a> (AAVE) is also largely non-rhotic and likely originated among enslaved Africans and African Americans influenced primarily by the non-rhotic, non-standard <a href="/wiki/Older_Southern_American_English" title="Older Southern American English">older Southern dialects</a>. A minority of linguists,<sup id="cite_ref-Word_on_the_Street_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Word_on_the_Street-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> contrarily, propose that AAVE mostly traces back to African languages spoken by the slaves who had to develop a <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Creole_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Creole English">Creole English</a> to communicate with slaves of other ethnic and linguistic origins.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2001_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2001-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> AAVE\'s important commonalities with Southern accents suggests it developed into a highly coherent and homogeneous variety in the 19th or early 20th century. AAVE is commonly stigmatised in North America as a form of "broken" or "uneducated" English, as are white Southern accents, but linguists today recognise both as fully developed varieties of English with their own norms shared by a large speech community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2002_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2002-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006b_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006b-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_and_New_Zealand">Australia and New Zealand</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand English</a></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:AustraliaPart2.ogg" title="File:AustraliaPart2.ogg">Speech example</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_7" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="1055.6828344671" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="AustraliaPart2.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/AustraliaPart2.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (93 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="93281" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a3/AustraliaPart2.ogg/AustraliaPart2.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="157600" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">An example of a male with a <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">general Australian accent</a>.</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Since 1788, English has been spoken in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a> has developed as a first language of the vast majority of the inhabitants of the Australian continent, its standard accent being <a href="/wiki/General_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="General Australian">General Australian</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">English of neighbouring New Zealand</a> has to a lesser degree become an influential standard variety of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEagleson1982_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEagleson1982-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Australian and New Zealand English are each other\'s closest relatives with few differentiating characteristics, followed by <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a> and the English of southeastern England, all of which have similarly non-rhotic accents, aside from some accents in the <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a> of New Zealand. Australian and New Zealand English stand out for their innovative vowels: many short vowels are fronted or raised, whereas many long vowels have diphthongised. Australian English also has a contrast between long and short vowels, not found in most other varieties. Australian English grammar aligns closely to British and American English; like American English, collective plural subjects take on a singular verb (as in <i>the government is</i> rather than <i>are</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200216\xe2\x80\x9321_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200216\xe2\x80\x9321-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurridge2010_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurridge2010-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> New Zealand English uses front vowels that are often even higher than in Australian English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200224\xe2\x80\x9326_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200224\xe2\x80\x9326-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaclagan2010_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaclagan2010-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordonCampbellHay_et_al.2004_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordonCampbellHay_et_al.2004-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Africa.2C_the_Caribbean.2C_and_South_Asia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa,_the_Caribbean,_and_South_Asia">Africa, the Caribbean, and South Asia</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_English" title="Nigerian English">Nigerian English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_English" title="Caribbean English">Caribbean English</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:South_African_English.ogg" title="File:South African English.ogg">Speech example</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_8" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="56.23873015873" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="South_African_English.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/South_African_English.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (87 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="87278" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/68/South_African_English.ogg/South_African_English.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="139416" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">An example of a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">black male</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">South African accent</a>.</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>English is spoken widely in South Africa and is an official or co-official language in several countries. In <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, English has been spoken since 1820, co-existing with <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaans language">Afrikaans</a> and various African languages such as the <a href="/wiki/Khoe_languages" title="Khoe languages">Khoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a>. Today about 9 percent of the South African population speak <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a> (SAE) as a first language. SAE is a non-rhotic variety, which tends to follow RP as a norm. It is alone among non-rhotic varieties in lacking intrusive r. There are different L2 varieties that differ based on the native language of the speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELanham1982_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELanham1982-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Most phonological differences from RP are in the vowels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass2002_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass2002-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Consonant differences include the tendency to pronounce /p, t, t\xcd\xa1\xca\x83, k/ without aspiration (e.g. <i>pin</i> pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p\xc9\xaan]</span> rather than as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[p\xca\xb0\xc9\xaan]</span> as in most other varieties), while r is often pronounced as a flap <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xbe]</span> instead of as the more common fricative.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200230\xe2\x80\x9331_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah200230\xe2\x80\x9331-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie_bbc_radio4_front_row_03_05_2013.flac" title="File:Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie bbc radio4 front row 03 05 2013.flac">Speech example</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_9" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="37.08225" data-startoffset="0" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="94704" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e2/Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie_bbc_radio4_front_row_03_05_2013.flac/Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie_bbc_radio4_front_row_03_05_2013.flac.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="192248" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie_bbc_radio4_front_row_03_05_2013.flac" type="audio/flac" data-title="Original FLAC file (1.32 Mbps)" data-shorttitle="FLAC source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="1323955" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">An example of a woman with an educated <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_English" title="Nigerian English">Nigerian accent</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chimamanda_Adichie" class="mw-redirect" title="Chimamanda Adichie">Chimamanda Adichie</a>)</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Nigerian English is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_dialects_of_the_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="List of dialects of the English language">dialect of English</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> It is based on British English, but in recent years, because of influence from the United States, some words of American English origin have made it into Nigerian English. Additionally, some new words and collocations have emerged from the language, which come from the need to express concepts specific to the culture of the nation (e.g. <i>senior wife</i>). Over 150 million population of Nigerians speak English.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several varieties of English are also spoken in the Caribbean Islands that were colonial possessions of Britain, including Jamaica, and the <a href="/wiki/Leeward_Islands" title="Leeward Islands">Leeward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windward_Islands" title="Windward Islands">Windward Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>. Each of these areas are home both to a local variety of English and a local English based creole, combining English and African languages. The most prominent varieties are <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_English" title="Jamaican English">Jamaican English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_English_Creole" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamaican English Creole">Jamaican Creole</a>. In Central America, English based creoles are spoken in on the Caribbean coasts of Nicaragua and Panama.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawton1982_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawton1982-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Locals are often fluent both in the local English variety and the local creole languages and <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switching</a> between them is frequent, indeed another way to conceptualise the relationship between Creole and Standard varieties is to see a spectrum of social registers with the Creole forms serving as "basilect" and the more RP-like forms serving as the "acrolect", the most formal register.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most Caribbean varieties are based on British English and consequently, most are non-rhotic, except for formal styles of Jamaican English which are often rhotic. Jamaican English differs from RP in its vowel inventory, which has a distinction between long and short vowels rather than tense and lax vowels as in Standard English. The diphthongs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ei/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ou/</span> are monophthongs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[e\xcb\x90]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[o\xcb\x90]</span> or even the reverse diphthongs <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ie]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[uo]</span> (e.g. <i>bay</i> and <i>boat</i> pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b\xca\xb2e\xcb\x90]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[b\xca\xb7o\xcb\x90t]</span>). Often word-final consonant clusters are simplified so that "child" is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcd\xa1\xca\x83ail]</span> and "wind" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[win]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002117\xe2\x80\x9318_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002117\xe2\x80\x9318-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawton1982256\xe2\x80\x9360_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawton1982256\xe2\x80\x9360-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115\xe2\x80\x9316_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah2002115\xe2\x80\x9316-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="659575" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">An example of a <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North Indian</a> woman with an <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian accent</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vandana_Shiva" title="Vandana Shiva">Vandana Shiva</a>)</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>As a historical legacy, <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a> tends to take RP as its ideal, and how well this ideal is realised in an individual\'s speech reflects class distinctions among Indian English speakers. Indian English accents are marked by the pronunciation of phonemes such as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span> (often pronounced with retroflex articulation as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x88]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x96]</span>) and the replacement of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xce\xb8/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xb0/</span> with dentals <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t\xcc\xaa]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d\xcc\xaa]</span>. Sometimes Indian English speakers may also use spelling based pronunciations where the silent &#x27e8;h&#x27e9; found in words such as <i>ghost</i> is pronounced as an Indian <a href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Breathy voice">voiced aspirated</a> stop <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1\xca\xb1]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESailaja200919\xe2\x80\x9324_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESailaja200919\xe2\x80\x9324-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist"
